    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer804781148724395083">The Genealogy of Morals consists of three essays exploring morality and its origins where Nietzsche makes ample use of his training as a philologist. These works contain Nietzsche's most thorough and clear expression of his psychological philosophy. This edition includes Ecce Homo, Nietzsche's review of his life and works, with the exception of The Antichrist. These two bo</span>
  <span id="freeText804781148724395083" style="display:none">The Genealogy of Morals consists of three essays exploring morality and its origins where Nietzsche makes ample use of his training as a philologist. These works contain Nietzsche's most thorough and clear expression of his psychological philosophy. This edition includes Ecce Homo, Nietzsche's review of his life and works, with the exception of The Antichrist. These two books are compiled, translated and annotated by renowned Nietzsche scholar Walter Kaufmann.</span>

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_244296208" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/244296208" />
    <a title="T.J. Beitelman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1482456-t-j-beitelman"><img alt="T.J. Beitelman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442518811p2/1482456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/244296208?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="T.J. Beitelman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="T.J. Beitelman" href="/user/show/1482456-t-j-beitelman">T.J. Beitelman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer244296208" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4740845649293554176">Make no mistake: Nietzsche was a nut. Bertrand Russell famously dismissed him as a megalomaniac, and maybe that’s true. People blame the Nazis on him, they say he was a misogynist, and on and on. I don’t really know about all that, one way or another (though the Nazi thing is demonstrably false — Nietzsche consistently rails against all things German, especially what he considered the Germanic tendency toward mindless group-think. He was also vehemently opposed to anti-Semitism. Maybe a Nazi or </span>
  <span id="freeText4740845649293554176" style="display:none">Make no mistake: Nietzsche was a nut. Bertrand Russell famously dismissed him as a megalomaniac, and maybe that’s true. People blame the Nazis on him, they say he was a misogynist, and on and on. I don’t really know about all that, one way or another (though the Nazi thing is demonstrably false — Nietzsche consistently rails against all things German, especially what he considered the Germanic tendency toward mindless group-think. He was also vehemently opposed to anti-Semitism. Maybe a Nazi or two misconstrued and/or appropriated his ideas, but it’s very likely he would’ve ended up in Dauchau had he been around).<br /><br />At any rate, there’s no question the guy cracked up and spent the last few years of his life as a catatonic and drooling shade of himself, being looked after by a sister he hated and mocked when he’d had his wits about him. If that aint a cautionary tale re: the risks of spending your life thinking heady thoughts, what is? (Good thing me and the Anonymous Sister are on good terms…)<br /><br />Still. Just cuz you’re crazy don’t mean you aint onto something. (Which is pretty much a direct quote from ol’ Freddie himself. You know. In translation.)<br /><br /><i>On the Genealogy of Morals</i> has stuck in my craw for a few reasons: <b>1)</b> a madcap, brilliant professor-type introduced me to it. He was great because he was the type of madcap, brilliant professor who could work the alchemical magic of turning an inscrutable text into a sacred one but — and I witnessed this — you put him in a grocery store and tell him to find the vanilla extract and he quickly comes to his wit’s end. Something about that strikes me as being exactly the way it should be; <b>2)</b> it’s about the roots of asceticism and how asceticism is and always will be rooted in hedonism, how Yes! and No! do their necessary little dance. (This is a dance I must admit I know at least a little too well.); <b>3)</b> Also: how there’s a difference between Yes! vs. No! and Right vs. Wrong. Not just a difference but a sequence: Yes! comes first. Then comes No! Then come the qualifiers.<br /><br />This is the important thing, though: Nietzsche is, first and foremost, an artist. A poet, in fact. (“Nut” / “Poet;” “Tomato” / “Tomahto.”) His background as a philologist is not a tangent to who and what he was. You can’t expect science or fact or consistency from him. He is interested in bigger things than just that. His language is beautiful and his metaphorical leaps are exhilarating. That’s where his Truth resides.<br /><br />The good thing about Nietzsche is you can pick him up, open to any page, and read a few sentences to chew on for several hours, days, or years. You don’t even have to understand him. Just be with him as if he was your crazy uncle. <br /><br />Everyone needs a crazy uncle. <br /><br />In fact, Nietzsche might say that everyone needs to be a crazy uncle. Or at any rate this: if you’re a crazy uncle, aint no use pretending otherwise. <br /><br /><i>Du sollst werden, der du bist</i>: You must become what you are.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4740845649293554176" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_30795476" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/30795476" />
    <a title="Joshua Nomen-Mutatio" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1447864-joshua-nomen-mutatio"><img alt="Joshua Nomen-Mutatio" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363779897p2/1447864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/30795476?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joshua Nomen-Mutatio" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joshua Nomen-Mutatio" href="/user/show/1447864-joshua-nomen-mutatio">Joshua Nomen-Mutatio</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1447864-joshua-nomen-mutatio?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1447864-joshua-nomen-mutatio?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1447864-joshua-nomen-mutatio?shelf=nietzsche">nietzsche</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1447864-joshua-nomen-mutatio?shelf=cultural-and-or-political">cultural-and-or-political</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1447864-joshua-nomen-mutatio?shelf=essays">essays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1447864-joshua-nomen-mutatio?shelf=ethics-and-or-metaethics">ethics-and-or-metaethics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1447864-joshua-nomen-mutatio?shelf=religion">religion</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30795476" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6640481190677456854">Here Nietzsche returns to the form of the essay after several complete works largely composed aphoristically. The second essay in the polemic <i>On the Geneology of Morals</i> is excellent and my personal favorite of the three essays that comprise this work. He discusses the historical tossings and turnings that have led to weird inversions of moral standards throughout the ages. The ways in which many eggs are often broken to make various omelettes and how the omelettes often turn out much differently</span>
  <span id="freeText6640481190677456854" style="display:none">Here Nietzsche returns to the form of the essay after several complete works largely composed aphoristically. The second essay in the polemic <i>On the Geneology of Morals</i> is excellent and my personal favorite of the three essays that comprise this work. He discusses the historical tossings and turnings that have led to weird inversions of moral standards throughout the ages. The ways in which many eggs are often broken to make various omelettes and how the omelettes often turn out much differently than intended. Social psychology at its most fearless and polemicized. <br /><br /><i>Ecce Homo</i> (tr. "Behold the man!" in reference to Pontius Pilate's presentation of Jesus to the blood thirsty crowd) is interesting as well. Nietzsche gives several short "reviews" of each of his own books written up until that time, some are a bit forgettable, some a bit more interesting. For a good example of official self-critique see his essay ("Attempt at Self-Criticism") about his first book <i>The Birth of Tragedy</i> which can be found in the intro to some copies of the same book. <br /><br />The rest of this Beholding of the Man consists of four short chapters entitled "Why I Am So Wise", "Why I Am So Clever", "Why I Write Such Good Books", and "Why I Am a Destiny". These are probably best read as something written on the brink of insanity and steeped in deliberate irony and sarcasm--but not completely. I'll just admit that I had a hard time taking much of it all that seriously. For several pages Nietzsche goes on about his ideas concerning nutrition. He also equates drinking alcohol with subscribing to Christianity. It's a bit of a laugh riot from some angles but one that includes a series of doubtful and perplexed moments about from where or why the laughter comes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6640481190677456854" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10010887656248733477" style="display:none">One of the few books that absolutely changed my life, and filled in as something not unlike a spiritual guide (between a time-gap following my denouncing formal religion, then not knowing how to proceed with philosophy as a "spiritual endeavor," which is how many "Eastern" philosophers define spirituality, by the way...)...<br /><br />Although any of Nietzsche's works might fit this bill (most would recommend Zarathustra), for some reason--probably due to my innate interest in the etymological significance of words as they relate to and/or have the capacity to transcend the sphere of mere labels into the territory of what Marx (via David Harvey's reading of him) might describe as their "fetish" character*; that is, Nietzsche's presentation offers insight into one way in which words get reified, or "essentialized." For this reason, it is one of his most important works (On Truth and Lie in the Extramoral Sense" essay would be the other); even though it isn't his most enjoyable read; perhaps for the same reason; e.g., it's fundamentally intellectual nature. <br /><br /><br />*Despite this analogy, to my knowledge, this work of Nietzsche's draws no overt connection with Marx.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_34781377" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/34781377" />
    <a title="Mr." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1596746-mr"><img alt="Mr." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1225752286p2/1596746.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/34781377?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mr." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mr." href="/user/show/1596746-mr">Mr.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer34781377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1535476682159730685">Nietzsche's complex sequel to Beyond Good and Evil is a remarkable achievement of philosophy, philology, and history. It laid the groundwork for such 20th century thinkers as Foucault and Deleuze, though they would never reach Nietzsche's complexity and moral sophistication. In the preface to the book, Nietzsche proposes the project of investigating the origins of morality on the grounds that human beings are unknown to themselves. He is ultimately concerned with the development of moral prejudi</span>
  <span id="freeText1535476682159730685" style="display:none">Nietzsche's complex sequel to Beyond Good and Evil is a remarkable achievement of philosophy, philology, and history. It laid the groundwork for such 20th century thinkers as Foucault and Deleuze, though they would never reach Nietzsche's complexity and moral sophistication. In the preface to the book, Nietzsche proposes the project of investigating the origins of morality on the grounds that human beings are unknown to themselves. He is ultimately concerned with the development of moral prejudices, and the value of morality itself. He criticizes mankind in its acceptance of moral principles, and writes: "we need a critique of moral values, the value of these values themselves must first be called in question-and for that there is needed a knowledge of the conditions and circumstances in which they grew, under which they evolved and changed" (456). <br />Nietzsche begins the essay (Good and Evil, Good and Bad), with a philological examination of the words and roots of the words related to good and evil, and a delimitation of their evolution. He makes a connection between the creations of words and places them within the historical context of rulers and nobility. Linguistically, Nietzsche has discovered that the `good' is linked with nobility. He writes: "everywhere `noble,' `aristocratic' in the social sense, is the basic concept from which `good' in the sense of `with aristocratic soul,' `noble,'" (464). Alternatively, words associated with the `bad' invariably were linked with the `plain,' `simple,' and `low.' In this way, morality as a human construction is an extension of power, wealth, and civilization. The origin of evil is intertwined with priestly aristocracies. <br />Nietzsche moves into a discussion of a shift in the history of morality, in which the morality of the priestly aristocracy is superceded by Jewish morality. For Nietzsche, the Jews inverted the morality of nobility and established a system which places value on the lower order of mankind. He indicates that the Jews believed "the wretched alone are the good; the poor, impotent, lowly alone are the good; the suffering, deprived, sick, ugly alone are pious, alone are blessed by God" (470). Nietzsche describes this turn as `the slave revolt' of morality. He describes the triumph of Judeo-Christian morality over the previous system of values, and indicates that this turn is a triumph for the herd instinct, and for ressentiment. He writes: "The slave revolt in morality begins when ressentiment itself becomes creative and gives birth to values: the ressentiment of natures that are denied the true reaction, that of deeds, and compensate themselves with an imaginary revenge" (472). Noble morality develops as an affirmation of itself, while slave morality always says No to what is external to it. For Nietzsche, the need to constantly turn outward to an external `other' and place judgment on it is the essence of ressentiment. <br />In the proceeding section of the treatise, Nietzsche discusses civilization's taming of man the animal. Here he writes: "Supposing that what is at any rate believed to be the `truth' really is true, and the meaning of all culture is the reduction of the beast of prey `man' to a tame and civilized animal, a domestic animal, then one would undoubtedly have to regard all those instincts of reaction and ressentiment through whose aid the noble races and their ideal were finally confounded and overthrown as the actual instruments of culture" (478). Nietzsche insists that Europe's taming of man is a tremendous danger, for we are made to be weary of our own being. For Nietzsche, this weariness and fear of man has compelled us to lose our love for him, to turn our backs on our instincts, to reject affirmation. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1535476682159730685" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_34781377'><a id="like_count_review_34781377" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/34781377?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">5 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F132626.On_the_Genealogy_of_Morals_Ecce_Homo">Like</a></span>
&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a href="/review/show/34781377?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1">see review</a>
    </div>


    <div id='comments_for_review_34781377' style='display: none;'>
</div>

<div id="review_735765504" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/735765504" />
    <a title="Kenna Day" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8689853-kenna-day"><img alt="Kenna Day" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1402846837p2/8689853.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/735765504?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kenna Day" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kenna Day" href="/user/show/8689853-kenna-day">Kenna Day</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8689853-kenna-day?shelf=german-lit">german-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8689853-kenna-day?shelf=critical-theory">critical-theory</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8689853-kenna-day?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8689853-kenna-day?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8689853-kenna-day?shelf=religious">religious</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer735765504" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4245021272480350350">Nietzsche is like a long lost friend to me. I read Zarathustra in high school and I remember connecting so deeply to his dissatisfaction with religion. Granted, I grew out of my flaming violent antitheism. But Nietzsche takes me back. <br/><br/>My favorite part regards slave morality in essay 1 of On the Genealogy of Morals. He talks about the structure of noble morality, in which strength and power and wealth-all aspects of nobility-are "good." And all else is bad. Slave morality is simply a reaction to </span>
  <span id="freeText4245021272480350350" style="display:none">Nietzsche is like a long lost friend to me. I read Zarathustra in high school and I remember connecting so deeply to his dissatisfaction with religion. Granted, I grew out of my flaming violent antitheism. But Nietzsche takes me back. <br /><br />My favorite part regards slave morality in essay 1 of On the Genealogy of Morals. He talks about the structure of noble morality, in which strength and power and wealth-all aspects of nobility-are "good." And all else is bad. Slave morality is simply a reaction to that noble morality. Slaves (anyone not in power) perceive a hostile environment and, as self protection, determine that which is not themselves (the nobles) must be evil. Nietzsche cites the Hebrew revolt as example. By determining enslavement as good, slaves decide their weakness is a true strength. (The meek shall inherit the earth.)<br /><br />And there you have it: my view of the power of religious brainwashing.<br /><br />Note: the binary flipping of "good and bad" and "good and evil" feels like a Derrida deconstruction, doesn't it?</span>
  <a data-text-id="4245021272480350350" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2393995994" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2393995994" />
    <a title="Aung Sett Kyaw Min" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52918291-aung-sett-kyaw-min"><img alt="Aung Sett Kyaw Min" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515573087p2/52918291.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2393995994?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aung Sett Kyaw Min" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aung Sett Kyaw Min" href="/user/show/52918291-aung-sett-kyaw-min">Aung Sett Kyaw Min</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2393995994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12241507032929723389">Nietzsche is truly a psychologist of the first rank. In this treatise, he ingeniously transforms the 'what' of morality into the 'who' of morality. Who are perpetrators of the value of value itself? And what can be said about their physiological condition? As always, Nietzsche's language of physiology <br/>is a joy to read and digest...<br/><br/>(1)<br/><br/>'Good' was a self-descriptive valuation of warriors who were possessed of healthy, vigorous and physical constitution, and an active capacity to forgive and forget </span>
  <span id="freeText12241507032929723389" style="display:none">Nietzsche is truly a psychologist of the first rank. In this treatise, he ingeniously transforms the 'what' of morality into the 'who' of morality. Who are perpetrators of the value of value itself? And what can be said about their physiological condition? As always, Nietzsche's language of physiology <br />is a joy to read and digest...<br /><br />(1)<br /><br />'Good' was a self-descriptive valuation of warriors who were possessed of healthy, vigorous and physical constitution, and an active capacity to forgive and forget the violence that was inflicted on them. They held no resentiment against their enemies, unable to take them seriously. The 'good' are self-centred, positing themselves as 'good' in advance. But they do not regard their enemies as bad or evil, only that they are not-good. <br /><br />On the other hand, a different kind of valuation is secreted by the enemies of the warrior class- the priestly, ascetic class. Physically sick, pale and morbid, their first creative deed is to posit the other class as 'bad' and 'evil' and only secondarily define themselves as 'good'. The best examplar of this valuation secreted by the downtrodden and the sick is the Christian religion, with Christ as the figure of the ascetic priest who administers this valuation as a palliative to the suffering masses.<br /><br />"Rome" against "Judea"; in Nietzsche's view, there is an ongoing conflict between the warrior class and the priestly. While the struggle is yet to be conclusively decided, it is abundantly clear that the Judean valuation has presently triumphed. That is, the priestly class has installed its value of values as the dominant valuation. After all, we have become very tame through the instruments of culture. But not all hope is lost. <br /><br />(2) <br /><br />'Justice' is essentially active, and functions to rein in the morbid excesses of the 'reactive' pathos, sublimating the resentiment of the reactive man from its object to the community as a whole. However it is futile to speak of justice "in itself" or injustice "in itself", since even the partial limitation of the will to power imposed by the institution of law goes against the will to power (justice is a means to acquiring greater power) <br /><br />Simply grasping the purpose of a concept or an institution does not suffice as an explanation as to its genesis; punishment for example is a concatenation of a whole host of uses and utilities and whose original purpose is buried under layers and layers of historical sedimentation. The sequence of procedures associated with 'punishment' therefore always already precedes our intentional conferral of meaning or purpose to it. <br /><br />What is the origin of bad conscience? Bad conscience is 'animal soul is turned on itself'. The formation of society curtailed the instincts of men who previously acted freely on their drives. Unable to discharge these instincts outwards, men began to project them inwards into a regime of self infliction and self mortalization. "Hostility, cruelty, joy in persecuting, in attacking, in change, in destruction-all this turned against the possessors of such instincts: that is the origin of the "bad conscience", concludes Nietzsche. Bad conscience is responsible for the Abrahamic conception of God, which in Nietzsche's opinion, amounts to man's self-laceration instead of his self valorization as in the case of the tribal gods. Is there a way out of 'bad conscience'? A redeemer with a vitalistic (life affirming) and immanent (oriented to the actual world) eye to morality must be on the distant horizon...<br /><br />(3)<br /><br />Is ascetic ideal necessarily a denial and repudiation of sensuality? In the case of a philosopher, the ascetic ideal amounts to "an optimum condition for the highest and boldest spirituality". However, it is unfortunate that the philosopher type has hitherto only able to survive by taking the posture and the appearance of the priestly type-world denying, hostile to life, suspicious of the senses, freed from sensuality, etc. In the end Nietzshe asks us rhetorically if the conditions of the current world are conducive to the emergence of the free spirit from the philosopher-priest caterpiller.<br /><br />The ascetic mode of valuation is truly 'monstrous' in so far as it subordinates all other valuations under it. It condemns the actual world to the effect to holding up the 'beyond' or the 'ideal' world as the beacon of truth. The ascetic priest offers a palliative to suffering through the neutralization of our affects and senses to the point of absolute indifference, and the sublimation of our consciousness to mechanical repetition in works and rituals. The ascetic priest neutralizes suffering by rendering it meaningful.<br /><br />But if the ascetic ideal is a will and an ideal nonetheless, then what is its possible counter-will or counter ideal? It is not scientific conscience, because it does not believe in itself sufficiently and above all still invests itself in a will to [disinterested] truth without offering a justification of this will, a will which it shares with the ascetic ideal. In the end, Nietzsche gestures towards the notion that the will to truth must overcome itself and in a stroke of self conciousness present itself the following question- "why the will to truth?" Man would rather will nothingness than not will at all...</span>
  <a data-text-id="12241507032929723389" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_19893514" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19893514" />
    <a title="Taylor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/987631-taylor"><img alt="Taylor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1209574429p2/987631.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19893514?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Taylor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Taylor" href="/user/show/987631-taylor">Taylor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19893514" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5601773216654943052">Far more mature than his furious work in 'Beyond Good and Evil', and really something to behold if you are willing to looking past the book's primary misgivings that arrive in the form of archaic thought. He rambles off the deep end in his meditations on the dangers of mixing not only race, but class in the next inevitably more mingled generations. These sentiments, however dated and faintly racist they may be, shouldn't take away from his general interest, that of the mechanisms of constraint i</span>
  <span id="freeText5601773216654943052" style="display:none">Far more mature than his furious work in 'Beyond Good and Evil', and really something to behold if you are willing to looking past the book's primary misgivings that arrive in the form of archaic thought. He rambles off the deep end in his meditations on the dangers of mixing not only race, but class in the next inevitably more mingled generations. These sentiments, however dated and faintly racist they may be, shouldn't take away from his general interest, that of the mechanisms of constraint imposed on the modern subject by virtue of their virtues. Nietzsche cleanly breaks down some notable differences between current and ancient religions, casting, at the time, new light on the realities of life under the cloud of morals, the ideas and structuring ability within the concepts of right and wrong. He is not calling for the abolishment of ethics or condoning murder, except once in a terrible but hypothetical sort of pre-fascist statement. What he is pointing out are the lengths to which modern morals and in particular modern religion, their loudspeaker, whip, and key, rule our thoughts and so our actions.<br /><br />Nietzsche is one of those thinkers who has been so digested that he may seem to be stating the obvious, but his status among the first to make these statements should be reason enough to read the book and touch a bit of the foundation on which so much modern thought has been set. I was raised quasi-religiously with an overactive thought process. It's a bad combination, answers don't suffice and it's said that thought should stand on faith. It leaves you (left me) feeling messed with, and Nietzsche's helped.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5601773216654943052" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer3549950160333860127">Of all Nietzsche's texts, the Genealogy is the most useful for students of theory and captures Nietzsche at his most systemic--interrogating the concepts of master and salve morality, ressentiment, asceticism, debt, schadenfreude, etc.,-- a contrast to the polemical aphorisms that contain only segments of a larger conceptual framework to be found in BGE and the Antichrist. <br/><br/>To fully appreciate Ecco Homo, as with TSZ, it should be read last, because it's essentially a commentary on his own oeuvre </span>
  <span id="freeText3549950160333860127" style="display:none">Of all Nietzsche's texts, the Genealogy is the most useful for students of theory and captures Nietzsche at his most systemic--interrogating the concepts of master and salve morality, ressentiment, asceticism, debt, schadenfreude, etc.,-- a contrast to the polemical aphorisms that contain only segments of a larger conceptual framework to be found in BGE and the Antichrist. <br /><br />To fully appreciate Ecco Homo, as with TSZ, it should be read last, because it's essentially a commentary on his own oeuvre as he was on the brink of delirium.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6547669703070835809">Interesting. While I don't agree with most of what Nietzsche posits, I appreciate the read to hear his perspective. Marx speaks with a greater darkness than Nietzsche, so the crazy hammering of the soul when evil is taught wasn't present for me here. I completely disagree with his ideas about the "ascetic priest," they sound closer to Korihor's philosophy (and what a sad end he came to - hmmm, very similar to Nietzsche's), because they're all recycled stories from the same author, the devil.   <span id="freeText6547669703070835809" style="display:none">Interesting. While I don't agree with most of what Nietzsche posits, I appreciate the read to hear his perspective. Marx speaks with a greater darkness than Nietzsche, so the crazy hammering of the soul when evil is taught wasn't present for me here. I completely disagree with his ideas about the "ascetic priest," they sound closer to Korihor's philosophy (and what a sad end he came to - hmmm, very similar to Nietzsche's), because they're all recycled stories from the same author, the devil. Oh wait, but there is no devil, right? THere is no good or evil, we're just told that so we're more tame, easier to control...funny, but anyone who ever goes down the path of carnal indulgence never finds happiness. <br /><br />His theory on the man of ressentiment is also interesting, although completely backwards. Nietzsche claims those who live life in peace and espouse Christian behavior are really weak and tell themselves they are stronger than those oppressing them because of their virtues, that one day they'll be greater than all of them, this couched in the beatitudes. He even addresses his sister who petitions him to see how difficult it is to keep the passions in control and allow the spirit to dominate the flesh, telling her how difficult it is to be alone, think these "new" thoughts and go forward in it without any support. THIS, of course, is far more difficult. Interesting ideas. </span>

  <span id="freeText3914128940855042171" style="display:none">From the section "Why I am so Wise":<br /><br /> "What is it, fundamentally, that allows us to recognize <i>who has turned out well</i>? That well-turned-out person pleases our senses, that he is carved from wood that is hard, delicate, and at the same time smells good. He has a taste only for what is good for him; his pleasure, his delight cease where the measure of what is good for him is transgressed. He guesses what remedies avail against what is harmful; he exploits bad accidents to his advantage; what does not kill him makes him stronger. instinctively, he collects from everything he sees, hears, lives through, <i>his</i> sum: he is a principle of selection, he discards much. He is always in his own company, whether he associates with books, human beings, or landscapes: he honors by <i>choosing</i>, by <i>admitting</i>, by <i>trusting</i>. He reacts slowly to all kinds of stimuli, with that slowness which long caution and deliberate pride have bred in him: he examines the stimulus that approaches him, he is far from meeting it halfway. He believes neither in 'misfortune' nor in 'guilt': he comes to terms with himself, with others; he knows how to <i>forget</i>--he is strong enough; hence everything must turn out for his best.<br /> "Well then, I am the <i>opposite</i> of a decadent, for I have just described <i>myself</i>." <br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_2591189362" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2591189362" />
    <a title="Mεδ Rεδħα" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61033954-m-r"><img alt="Mεδ Rεδħα" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577485165p2/61033954.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2591189362?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mεδ Rεδħα" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mεδ Rεδħα" href="/user/show/61033954-m-r">Mεδ Rεδħα</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61033954-m-r?shelf=nietzsche">nietzsche</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61033954-m-r?shelf=philosophie">philosophie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61033954-m-r?shelf=les-inoubliables">les-inoubliables</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2591189362" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3110592252437395519">"The Genealogy of Morals" is articulated in three dissertations: "the good and the bad", "the resentment" and "the ascetic ideals". It seems that this essay is one of the most accessible Nietzsche...difficult for me to compare, since this is the first book of the author that I read, but I must admit that I had to hang on at times, I am not a philosopher by training (but are not we all a little by nature, finally?).<br/><br/>Nevertheless, this reading turns out to be very interesting and at first very rema</span>
  <span id="freeText3110592252437395519" style="display:none">"The Genealogy of Morals" is articulated in three dissertations: "the good and the bad", "the resentment" and "the ascetic ideals". It seems that this essay is one of the most accessible Nietzsche...difficult for me to compare, since this is the first book of the author that I read, but I must admit that I had to hang on at times, I am not a philosopher by training (but are not we all a little by nature, finally?).<br /><br />Nevertheless, this reading turns out to be very interesting and at first very remarkable for the style...Mr Nietzsche knew, without any doubt, how to handle the words. I will not engage in a long criticism (impure reason) but some points of the argument tickle me, when others convince me.<br /><br />I understand the distinction between the morality of slaves (a morality of resentment towards the masters) and that of the "masters". For all that, the author never raises the question of the legitimacy of the dominant / dominated relationship (nor therefore of its perpetuation) for the simple reason that it is inept for him: the lambs can not blame the birds of prey for being what they are. There are therefore strong and weak "by nature" and Nietzsche opposes "the false slogan" of the morality of resentment (the privilege of the majority) to that of the aristocracy, "frightening and enchanting", but without further development (at the end of the first dissertation). He also presents Napoleon as the last man to have incarnated this aristocratic ideal, a synthesis of inhuman and superhuman.<br /><br />As far as religion is concerned, Nietzsche prunes the Christian morality, which by "the morality of morals" shapes a society that emphasizes adaptation to contingencies instead of valorizing the will to power, that is to say activity, conquering forces, aggressive, innovative. The Nietzschean man (the superman to come) is instinctive and does not intend to excuse it while morality seeks to force him to do so and to give up the pleasure of suffering. Indeed, for Nietzsche it is not the suffering that poses a problem but rather its lack of meaning and that is why men created the gods that engendered "the priestly aristocracy" who imagined this horror that is the peach. Basically, if you suffer it's your fault man. But do not worry, if you are a good Christian you can go to heaven after...<br /><br />A logic, we can easily imagine, that does not favor the aristocracy warrior, the "masters", those who "can promise Because, confident of their abilities, they will keep their promise. One can feel the parallels with Freud (notably "Malaise in Civilization"): the morality of morals recalls the superego and the instincts the impulses, except that Nietzsche does not see the positive aspects of the first, nor the tyrannical potential of the latter. In fact, the Nietzschean man is a little neo-liberal before the hour, a guy who wants the right uninhibited, without the moral of holy water.<br /><br />A word about the supposed links between Nazism and Nietzsche: yes, he sometimes uses examples where the word race is at the party (with eugenic sights), yes he tends not to be very cool with the Jews because he sees there "The priestly people par excellence" but at the same time he does not like the anti-Semites and does not appear to me at all as nationalist, he is not always tender with the Germans...From there to say that he would have has been recovered there is only one step (that I will leave to each one the care to cross or not) which, in the end, would only accredit his ideas on the historical dynamics...<br /><br />Finally Nietzsche gives philosophy the laudable goal of criticizing values ​​and morality (to hear here a Christian morality) but without specifying for what purpose. And for good reason ! If he asks the question of the ideal it is only to recall all that it cost to humanity during the history and to refute it en bloc. On this point, it is difficult for me to conceive of a man without ideal...It may be objected that the FUTURE has given Nietzsche reason when we see what communism has given...But perhaps it is the betrayal of ideals which would be to deplore more than the ideals in themselves.<br /><br />There also Friedrich has answer to everything since, this world not being (or having been) a conglomerate of "wills of power" in struggle, the ideas Since their birth, concepts, inventions, etc... are diverted from their initial goals to be "rearranged" by a superior will...So there is no simplistic cause-and-effect relationship in the historical dynamic according to Nietzsche...&amp; since God is only a deadly fable (as much as the scientific atheism which is the flip side of the coin, since he is simply trying to make the ideal more credible), he comes to the hypothesis that he there is no truth and everything is possible...<br /><br />...But does the non-definitive, non-universal character of the truth, in short, the fact that it is CONSTRUCTED, make it possible to think that everything is possible? And if, like Mathieu Potte-Bonneville, it was reduced to its essence by affirming "that there is truth only in the eclipse of the masters"?<br /><br />I wanted to finish with the usual precautions, of the kind, you Nietzsche's specialists, do not start to jump on me, I am only a neophyte HUMBLE who gives you his look...But since I suddenly feel the soul of a superman I just want to say...</span>
  <a data-text-id="3110592252437395519" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3006595928" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3006595928" />
    <a title="Greg Hickey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28503927-greg-hickey"><img alt="Greg Hickey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438201191p2/28503927.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3006595928?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg Hickey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg Hickey" href="/user/show/28503927-greg-hickey">Greg Hickey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3006595928" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17250334499935256602">This might be my favorite volume of Nietzsche’s work. The first part, On the Genealogy of Morals demonstrates some of his most straightforward writing, with a carefully developed study of the development of what we call morality. Nietzsche balances his attacks on the Judeo-Christian morality of “the meek shall inherit the earth” with a dispassionate assessment of the necessity and beneficial side effects of this development. All told, it is an original and provocative essay from a one-of-a-kind </span>
  <span id="freeText17250334499935256602" style="display:none">This might be my favorite volume of Nietzsche’s work. The first part, On the Genealogy of Morals demonstrates some of his most straightforward writing, with a carefully developed study of the development of what we call morality. Nietzsche balances his attacks on the Judeo-Christian morality of “the meek shall inherit the earth” with a dispassionate assessment of the necessity and beneficial side effects of this development. All told, it is an original and provocative essay from a one-of-a-kind philosopher. <br /><br />The second part, Ecce Homo, is both bombastic (with chapter titles like “Why I Am So Wise,” “Why I Am So Clever” and “Why I Write Such Good Books”) and an insightful look at the interplay between Nietzsche’s life and his work. And for those who would misinterpret Nietzsche’s ideas as a call to nationalism and racial purity, his screeds against nationalism, German culture and anti-Semitism make his true feelings very clear. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17250334499935256602" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_495766534" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/495766534" />
    <a title="Shawn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3921446-shawn"><img alt="Shawn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321850411p2/3921446.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/495766534?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shawn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shawn" href="/user/show/3921446-shawn">Shawn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3921446-shawn?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3921446-shawn?shelf=essays">essays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer495766534" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4329713482556140866"><blockquote>"I find it difficult to write a review of a philosophical work; difficult because it is initially put upon the reviewer to agree or disagree with an idea, but one must first summarize--and by doing that, one has already levied judgment." -me</blockquote> I wrote that passage on the back page of my copy of this text. The page number I referenced before writing this thought is page 326, which contains the quote from <i>Ecce Homo</i> (1900): "I have a terrible fear that one day I will be pronounced <i>holy</i>: you will gues</span>
  <span id="freeText4329713482556140866" style="display:none"><blockquote>"I find it difficult to write a review of a philosophical work; difficult because it is initially put upon the reviewer to agree or disagree with an idea, but one must first summarize--and by doing that, one has already levied judgment." -me</blockquote> I wrote that passage on the back page of my copy of this text. The page number I referenced before writing this thought is page 326, which contains the quote from <i>Ecce Homo</i> (1900): "I have a terrible fear that one day I will be pronounced <i>holy</i>: you will guess why I publish this book <i>before</i>; it shall prevent people from doing mischief with me" (emphasis original). Walter Kaufmann, the translator, notes that <i>Ecce Homo</i> was not published until 1908, eight years after Friedrich Nietzsche's death; eight years after Peter Gast proclaimed at the funeral of his friend: "Holy be thy name to all coming generations" (326). I find this first quote significant for many reasons, and it is the one I will deal with during the entirety of this review.<br /><br />First, it is important to note what I am reading. This text is one of Nietzsche's final works. In fact, as the note states, it was published posthumously--not the classical way of familiarizing oneself with a great philosopher, of this I am aware. My interest with Nietzsche began long ago through references by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1260.Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault" rel="nofollow noopener">Michel Foucault</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4132.Jacques_Derrida" title="Jacques Derrida" rel="nofollow noopener">Jacques Derrida</a>, and <a href="http://womens-studies.rutgers.edu/faculty/core-faculty/143-jasbir-puar" rel="nofollow noopener">Jasbir Puar</a>, but the first actual text of his that I encountered was: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/83288.A_Nietzsche_Reader" title="A Nietzsche Reader by Friedrich Nietzsche" rel="nofollow noopener">A Nietzsche Reader</a>. This text is organized thematically, and is a good primer for Nietzsche's writing. That being noted, I began this text with the later <i>Ecce Homo</i>, <i>not</i> the earlier <i>On The Genealogy of Morals</i>. The former text contains Nietzsche's personal account of his own writing; from <i>The Birth of Tragedy</i> (1872) to <i>The Case of Wagner</i> (1888), and with everything else in between. But I was not reading it for that textual investigation; I was reading it because of the "Why I Am" essays.<br /><br />You may have heard of them ("Why I Am So Wise," "Why I Am So Clever," etc.), or maybe you passed them in the bookstore and thought "What a pompous <i>ass</i> this Nietzsche fellow is!" I guess so. But I read them because I thought myself clever as well. Actually, I had made this same statement to two people on New Year's Day 2013; namely, "I am so wise," spoken with earnest pride. Then the seed was planted, and I went to the local used bookstore the next day to purchase this text after having seen it there over one month prior.<br /><br />I think one ought to approach Nietzsche in this manner: with a positive interest and happy conjectures, seeking gainful contemplation. Without that, one may happen upon an unhappy and lonely man writing manifestos for the Third Reich; but if that is the case, then one has read foolishly and done grave mischief to Nietzsche. This is a quite specific mischief, and it is settled throughout <i>Ecce Homo</i>. If anything, one should take away from this text Nietzsche's <i>disgust</i> for the Germans, his absolute <i>abhorrence</i> of nationalism, and his utmost desire to be understood. I shall lift another quotation circa 1900: "let the Germans commit one more immortal blunder in relation to me that will stand in all eternity" (<i>Ecce Homo</i> 324). Wie Stören...<br /><br />But I digress.<br /><br />For me, reading Nietzsche isn't about a grand idea or accumulating argumentative munitions against religion or morality, but about approaching art and life with a new and refreshing understanding. I cannot imagine attempting to decipher <em>exactly</em> what point or lesson or utilitarian application the reader was supposed to infer from Nietzsche's master/slave morality detailed in <em>On The Genealogy of Morals</em>, but I can apply his thesis on <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ressentiment" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">ressentiment</a> </em>to the integration of the tragic figure into popular culture, starting with Arthur Miller's essay on his own <em>
  <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Death_of_a_Salesman" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Death of a Salesman</a>
</em> (1949), entitled "<a href="http://www.nytimes.com/books/00/11/12/specials/miller-common.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Tragedy and The Common Man</a>" (written the same year). [Essay forthcoming]. It seems to me that Nietzsche is a referential adviser; not a person to whom one should read, digest and discard; nor an author whom one should carry in ones back pocket; but, rather, an author one should consider from time to time, if only to contemplate our world from a different angle.<br /><br />Many will advise not to approach Nietzsche lightly, but to consider him gravely and with steadfast measure. I disagree entirely. Approach Nietzsche when you are up, not down; when you have found happiness, not when you seek it; when you are ready to say YES to life, not when you feel it at its heaviest burden. Nietzsche <i>is</i> someone to be taken lightly; without weightlessness one cannot ascend to, and descend from, the great heights he offers, one cannot comprehend his Zarathustra, and one cannot read him free of mischief. So, with this in mind, you are sure to find answers in the divine human that is yourself, before you find them in the Nietzsche whom so many so desperately and despondently seek.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4329713482556140866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2218178099" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2218178099" />
    <a title="Tyler V." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54264220-tyler-v"><img alt="Tyler V." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563542616p2/54264220.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2218178099?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tyler V." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tyler V." href="/user/show/54264220-tyler-v">Tyler V.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2218178099" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14381366643797922252">There are two major works included in this volume. I read both twice. The first read was for comprehension. The second for fluidity of ideation and memorization. I think I have a decent understanding of both works, or at least as good of an understanding as anyone can achieve with the enigma that is Friedrich Nietzsche. Because there are two works here, I will review them each separately. However, my final 4/5 star rating is an amalgamation of my overall experience and take away. Translator and </span>
  <span id="freeText14381366643797922252" style="display:none">There are two major works included in this volume. I read both twice. The first read was for comprehension. The second for fluidity of ideation and memorization. I think I have a decent understanding of both works, or at least as good of an understanding as anyone can achieve with the enigma that is Friedrich Nietzsche. Because there are two works here, I will review them each separately. However, my final 4/5 star rating is an amalgamation of my overall experience and take away. Translator and educator Walter Kaufmann successfully creates a volume that is greater than the sum of its parts here: the combination of <i>On the Genealogy of Morals</i> and <i>Ecce Homo</i> is a radical learning experience that every reader, not just philosophy students, should try. Nietzsche makes philosophy fun and even when I completely disagree with him I am never bored. He helps me shape my own world of thought, even when it chafes against his. <br /><br /><i>
  <b>On the Genealogy of Morals</b>
</i><br />I have heard a lot of people say about this work that it is Nietzsche at his most coherent and sober, that he has a clear purpose in mind. I disagree. I found this work much more challenging than <i>Ecce Homo</i> or <i>Beyond Good &amp; Evil</i>. Although I will admit it is straightforward and direct in its goal. The work is broken up into three essays. <br /><br />The first is about Good and Bad vs. Good and Evil, or more commonly Master Morality vs. Slave Morality. While Nietzsche doesn't necessarily agree with the brutality of Master Morality he does dislike this reasoning less than he dislikes Slave Morality. He abhors pity, Christianity, guilt, resentment, anti-natural states of piety, etc. In this essay he comes to the conclusion that Judea (Christianity and Slave Morality) has beaten Rome (Master Morality, Dionysus, saying yes to our natural bodies and urges, sex, artistry, passion, pain), but not for good. Rome isn't out for the count. He is holding out hope for a return to "tragedy" as he calls it. He also comes to the radical conclusion that science, scholarship, and atheism are <i>not</i> the defeat of Christianity. They are merely the next form of it, closer to the real core of the issue. Instead of worshipping a God, they worship truth! They see truth as beyond criticism. When philosophers and scholars posit, they do so with a blind <i>assumption</i> that truth is the ultimate value. Nietzsche isn't necessarily saying that truth is bad, but rather that we should examine its value with a keener eye than philosophers of the past have. This is profound and like nothing that ever came before it.<br /><br />The second is about guilt and the "bad conscience." Why do human beings beat themselves up for past transgressions? Why do they beat themselves up for urges and states they cannot help? Why do we take all of the natural things inside ourselves (sex, lust, will to power, aggression, etc.) and bottle them up in favor of becoming more like the Christian God? Nietzsche attempts to tackle these questions with this essay. He concludes that the bad conscience stems from not being able to exorcise our cruelty. Man is naturally cruel, he argues. When he had to leave his wild jungle and wilderness to come into civilized society, he was no longer able to be cruel, especially once Christendom and Slave Morality took over. So, instead of being cruel to others as is natural, he turns the cruelty inward to himself. Thus the sickness of guilt. Nietzsche argues these states are not natural and actually harmful to the advancement of the human race. However, he does <i>not</i> argue that we should return to the jungle or base cruelty. He admits that civilized society made us more spiritual and was a necessary step for us.<br /><br />The third and final essay deals with the meaning of ascetic ideals. When he uses the term 'ascetic,' he doesn't necessarily mean our modern asceticism, like avoiding unhealthy food and exercising daily. He means ascetic in the Christian sense: chastity, poverty, humility, etc. He argues that the meaning of asceticism is going to be different and nuanced for an artist, a saint, a scholar, and a priest but that all of these types share one common distinction. Asceticism gives meaning to our suffering. Human beings accept suffering. We don't believe it could ever truly end. Even with modern comforts, we are all still depressed and anxious. In fact, many of us would not want suffering to stop because it makes us better. Nietzsche argues that we do, however, need a <i>meaning</i> for our suffering. We can't suffer for nothing. The Greeks gave meaning to their suffering by adding spectators: the tragic gods like Apollo and Dionysus. In Nietzsche's time though, we "will to nothing," i.e. decline the human race by trying to make ourselves like the Christian God. We denigrate and avoid our natural state, hence asceticism, in order to give our suffering a purpose and "higher" meaning. Nietzsche posits that there has not been a more damaging event to our overall wellbeing than this. Christian asceticism has ruined both art and health, he says. <br /><br />Overall, even when I disagreed with the conclusions, I found these essays to be a brilliant beam of light. They enlightened me in a world-historical context. I feel like I actually <i>learned</i> something here. It's rare to read a book in which the ideas presented are radically novel or unique to the reader. Often, rather, an author presents an idea you already know but writes it well enough to make you believe it is new. It is the same gift with new wrappings. Nietzsche offers an entirely new gift with <i>The Genealogy</i>, and nothing like it was seen on Earth before or after. Whether that is a good or bad thing is up to you and your perspective. 4/5<br /><br /><i>
  <b>Ecce Homo</b>
</i><br />This work is a sort of hybrid. In one sense it is Nietzsche's autobiography. Nietzsche on Nietzsche. Instead of merely telling the story of his life, though, he tells the story of his mind through a recap of all his books. In another sense it is an essay expounding on an idea Nietzsche developed and refined late in his career, that of amor fati (love of fate). He explains it better and more succinctly than I ever could: “My formula for greatness in a human being is amor fati: that one wants nothing to be different, not forward, not backward, not in all eternity. Not merely bear what is necessary, still less conceal it—all idealism is mendaciousness in the face of what is necessary—but love it." By that he means saying yes to life, even to all of its trappings and suffering. <br /><br />The entirety of <i>Ecce Homo</i> is an exercise in amor fati. Nietzsche suffered much in his life: near blindness, migraines, stomach issues, social alienation (the fault is mostly his own on that one), solitude, and not to mention seeing all of his friends become privy to fascist nationalism. Despite all of this and despite himself he still deeply loves life, he says yes to it, and would not change a single iota of his experience. This is a profound idea. I think it is a healthy philosophy to adopt.<br /><br />The work has some flaws. It spends too much time quoting <i>Thus Spoke Zarathustra</i>. When <i>Ecce Homo</i> was written, no one had read that work. So, Nietzsche tends to over-explain it here and a good 10% of the work is mere regurgitation of <i>Zarathustra</i>. <br /><br />Another flaw is that he spends far too much of the painfully small page-count denouncing Germany. While I have no problem reading a master philosopher and radical thinker tear down nationalism and racism, Nietzsche goes a bit too far in places. Even his own friends aren't excepted from his arrows of spite. I don't mind his arguments, but they rub me as contradictory to the man's previous philosophy (coincidentally presented in <i>The Genealogy</i> above) of overcoming resentment. <br /><br />It seems to me these caustic comments about Germany are full of resentment for a group of people never gave him enough attention. He mentions near the end of the book that none of his friends ever understood his work nor defended him from his German detractors. "I tell every one of my friends to his face that he has never considered it worthwhile to study any of my writings...[The Case of Wagner, Section 4, pg. 324 in the volume I am reviewing]" There are many throwaway quotes with a similar tone of resentment. Despite him mentioning this in a casual way, for repeat and astute readers such as myself, this is telling. His bile for Germany stems from the fact that they ignored his genius and left him defenseless. Nietzsche considered himself Polish and never identified with the German culture of the Reich. For this he is deeply brave. <br /><br />At the beginning of the work Nietzsche mentions that he is writing this autobiographer so that he will not be misunderstood or tampered with after he is gone ("Some are born posthumously" comes to mind). He knew he would achieve popularity and true admiration after his death, so he wished to set the record straight with <i>Ecce Homo</i> before that happened. Unfortunately, after a mental collapse, his sister took over his estate and tampered with the writings to make the works agreeable to her Nazi tendencies. She avoided publishing <i>Ecce Homo</i> until almost twenty years later, in 1908. She needed the autobiography to help write prefaces for the other works. It made her seem more insightful to Nietzsche's true character. So his deepest fear of misunderstanding and slander became true. He was posthumously tied with the nationale nervosó anti-Semitist values he so violently attacked and hated.<br /><br />Despite all of this, Nietzsche still finds a way to sing life's praises. He rarely ends a passage on a bad note. Rather, he preaches forgetting (not forgiving) one's enemies and one's mistakes. Learn from them! But never dwell on them. Perhaps thank an enemy rather than seek petty revenge, for you most likely learned something or gained strength. <br /><br />Amor fati teaches a noble strength of heart, a will to yes-saying, a will to the vigor of life! Nihilism is one of the deepest and most disgusting things that can happen to a person. This book is its anti-toxin par excellence. Despite its flaws, despite its troubling publishing history, despite its bile and resentment for everything German, and despite its too-oft repetition of <i>Zarathustra</i> it is a one-of-a-kind autobiography from one of the best writers and thinkers in history. It could be profound and life-altering for anyone! It certainly was for me. 4/5</span>
  <a data-text-id="14381366643797922252" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1382731929" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1382731929" />
    <a title="Kevin K" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12365026-kevin-k"><img alt="Kevin K" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584102054p2/12365026.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1382731929?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin K" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin K" href="/user/show/12365026-kevin-k">Kevin K</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12365026-kevin-k?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12365026-kevin-k?shelf=ethics">ethics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12365026-kevin-k?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1382731929" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17366076711211195791">This review only applies to <i>On the Genealogy of Morals</i> in this volume. Echoing Nick's review, I must say this book is far superior to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12321.Beyond_Good_and_Evil' title='Beyond Good and Evil by Friedrich Nietzsche' rel='nofollow noopener'>Beyond Good and Evil</a>. Here we have a tightly-focused Nietzsche in peak form, planting seeds that have grown into whole bodies of thought. Most obvious is Nietzsche's foreshadowing of Freud. Apparently Freud attributed to Nietzsche "more penetrating knowledge of himself than any man who ever lived or was likely to live"; Freud's biographer and acquaintance, Ernest </span>
  <span id="freeText17366076711211195791" style="display:none">This review only applies to <i>On the Genealogy of Morals</i> in this volume. Echoing Nick's review, I must say this book is far superior to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12321.Beyond_Good_and_Evil" title="Beyond Good and Evil by Friedrich Nietzsche" rel="nofollow noopener">Beyond Good and Evil</a>. Here we have a tightly-focused Nietzsche in peak form, planting seeds that have grown into whole bodies of thought. Most obvious is Nietzsche's foreshadowing of Freud. Apparently Freud attributed to Nietzsche "more penetrating knowledge of himself than any man who ever lived or was likely to live"; Freud's biographer and acquaintance, Ernest Jones, even claimed that Freud avoided reading Nietzsche due to worries about the similarity of their ideas. It was an innocent era before the 19th century, blissfully unaware of the subconscious mind. Morality was taken at face value, and there was no cold-eyed probing of its underlying/subconscious motives. Nietzsche was one of the first and most acute philosophers to expose the machinations hidden under the smooth facade. Previously my impression was that Nietzsche pioneered this style of thought, but I've learned through recent reading that Marx was earlier. The two are like bookends. Marx claims that bourgeois morality is just a cynical false-front that the bourgeoisie and upper classes use to brainwash the masses; Nietzsche, meanwhile, claims that slave morality is just a cynical false-front that the rabble use to brainwash the aristocracy and elites. I'm inclined to think they're both right! Another notable offshoot of the <i>Genealogy of Morals</i> is Michel Foucault's classic <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/119552.Discipline_and_Punish_The_Birth_of_the_Prison" title="Discipline and Punish The Birth of the Prison by Michel Foucault" rel="nofollow noopener">Discipline and Punish: The Birth of the Prison</a>.<br /><br />Today, evolution and sociobiology are fashionable topics, and there must be a dozen books out describing how morals are a product of the evolutionary process. A good example is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/346558.The_Evolution_of_Morality" title="The Evolution of Morality by Richard Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">The Evolution of Morality</a> by the philosopher Richard Joyce. Joyce's book is interesting but flawed for a couple of reasons. First, in order to show morality is a product of evolution, he has to make claims about events that happened millions of years ago, and there simply aren't any pertinent facts or evidence from that time. He ends up with a "just so story," as <a href="http://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2012/09/17/it-aint-necessarily-so" rel="nofollow noopener">Stephen J. Gould famously put it</a>. Joyce's fairy tales about what characteristics helped animals to survive a million years ago may be plausible, or even true, but we simply don't know what happened back then, so fairy tales they remain. The theory is closer in impulse to historical novel writing than to a genuine empirical science like biology. <br /><br />The most important reason that sociobiological moral theories fail, however, is that human morality simply isn't innate or programmed in by evolution. Emotions are biological, but morality proper is a <i>cultural</i> phenomenon which has gone through almost kaleidoscopic transformations over the course of history. That's Nietzsche's revolutionary insight. It's true that humans have emotional responses of empathy toward others; but it's also true that humans take pleasure in inflicting pain on others (and themselves), as Nietzsche points out. Has empathy been hard-wired into us by evolution? Very unlikely considering that the Romans tortured and murdered people as public entertainment for centuries, and no one batted an eye. It was completely normal, upstanding, moral behavior at that time.<br /><br />My conclusion is that Nietzsche was right on target, and today's sociobiologists are following a red herring. There's some, but not much, moral knowledge on the path they're following. The better path is the one Nietzsche opened up here: in-depth research into the historical evolution of morality as <i>culture</i>. Hopefully the promise of Nietzsche's work can be fulfilled in the Internet era. There's probably never been better conditions for ferreting out the strange moral histories of the world's tribes, civilizations and peoples. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17366076711211195791" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
