<div id="review_1804802697" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1804802697" />
    <a title="Brandon Sitch" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30154188-brandon-sitch"><img alt="Brandon Sitch" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1804802697?book_show_action=true">Jul 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brandon Sitch" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brandon Sitch" href="/user/show/30154188-brandon-sitch">Brandon Sitch</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30154188-brandon-sitch?shelf=german">german</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30154188-brandon-sitch?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1804802697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12374610766271169797">I’m not exactly sure how to categorize this book. It isn’t strictly a philosophical text, if you come to this after reading Kant, or Moore, or Hegel, you won’t recognize this as philosophy—and I tend to think <i>that</i> is more a criticism of modern philosophy than of Nietzsche—but the Genealogy is full of wisdom, originality, and beautiful writing. It also isn’t an ethical work, at least not in the usual sense, but rather a combination of a metaethical and historic approach. Nietzsche’s objective for</span>
  <span id="freeText12374610766271169797" style="display:none">I’m not exactly sure how to categorize this book. It isn’t strictly a philosophical text, if you come to this after reading Kant, or Moore, or Hegel, you won’t recognize this as philosophy—and I tend to think <i>that</i> is more a criticism of modern philosophy than of Nietzsche—but the Genealogy is full of wisdom, originality, and beautiful writing. It also isn’t an ethical work, at least not in the usual sense, but rather a combination of a metaethical and historic approach. Nietzsche’s objective for this work was summarized within the text: “Let us articulate this <i>new demand:</i> we need a <i>critique</i> of moral values, <i>the value of these values themselves must first be called in question</i>—and for that there is needed a knowledge of the conditions and circumstances in which they grew, under which they evolved and changed”. <br />	The book is split in to three essays titled: <i>“Good and Evil,” “Good and Bad”</i>; <i>“Guilt,” “Bad Conscience,” and the Like</i>; and <i>What is the Meaning of Ascetic Ideals?</i> But before one starts to analyze what the book is, it is important to understand what it is <i>not</i>. It is not filled with pro-German, anti-Semitic rhetoric and is not the how-to guide for cold-blooded murderers (Leopold and Loeb immediately come to mind), but it is also understandable that these misinterpretations have occurred. He does, at times, write negatively about ‘the Jews’ in such a way which makes it clear that it is more of an attack on Judaism than Jews (it’s also worth adding that he antagonizes almost everyone), but the sharpest criticisms are against anti-Semites. One problem with writing such powerful sentences is that those sentences tend to be lifted out of context as if they were meant to stand alone. This lets the reader take out of Nietzsche whatever message he or she wants and these incorrect interpretations reflects more on the reader than the writer. <br />	In the first essay, the important distinction between “Good and Evil” and “Good and Bad” is explored. He argues that at first there was only a noble and aristocratic notion of what it was meant to be good, including traits such as strength, power, and beauty. From this conception of the good arises the notion of the bad. The bad is taken to denote merely the absence of the good, the bad is not a moral judgment placed upon those without the qualities of the good, but rather a way to explain that those people lack these aristocratic/Homeric virtues. Being bad doesn’t mean that one is contemptible, but rather that they are insignificant. Further evidence is provided for this through etymology, the German words for bad and plain (schlecht and schlicht) are hypothesized by Nietzsche to come from the same root word, which would be evidence for his claim that they originally were synonymous. Then how, and why, did this sense of good and bad change? For Nietzsche, the answer is Judaism (though it is a charge that can be equally levied against Christianity) with what amounts to a slave revolt of morality. With Judaism an inversion of the concept of “good” occurred, such that all that was weak was to be heralded as good, and—what was worse and more pernicious—everything with which strength and nobility had been associated was now condemned as “evil”. The Jews who created this inversion were motivated by their <i>ressentiment</i>, which is suffering and pain derived from the good fortune of those who are powerful and admirable (the opposite of schadenfreude). He believes there was a clash between the two conceptions “Good and Evil” vs “Good and Bad” and that “Good and Evil” came to dominate western thought for millennia.<br />	The second essay involves two main points: what is the nature of the creditor-debtor relationship, and what are the trade-offs and conditions through which we can from societies. Both of which are important to the essay, but for the sake of brevity I will only cover the former. The creditor-debtor relationship should only be formed between two strong individuals, as they are the only one’s who are to some extent in control of their lives and can influence the outcomes. If you are too weak to control what happens in your life, how can you possibly predict the future enough to enter into a long-term agreement? This is not, however, to say that these contracts between strong individuals will always be upheld. When the debtor fails to repay what he owes, the creditor is allowed, perhaps obligated, to physically punish the debtor. The reasons for punishing them do not involve any reasons with which we are today familiar. It isn’t that what the debtor has done is shameful or sinful, or that this is to deter the debtor from entering into future agreements which he or she won’t be able to pay off, but rather the reason is simply since the creditor cannot get what they are owed, they will instead be repaid in the pleasure of punishing and dominating their debtor: “a recompense in the form of a kind of <i>pleasure</i>—the pleasure of being allowed to vent his power freely upon one who is powerless, the voluptuous pleasure <i>“de faire le mal pour le plaisir de le faire,”</i> the enjoyment of violation”. This notion is completely foreign to what the Western societies think of punishment and the repayment of debts, and Nietzsche’s version seems both more and less cruel than the way in which we currently treat punishment. While on its face the idea of dominating someone for the pleasure derived from that as an act of repayment seems sadistic, it is viewed as simply an economic exchange. There is no judgment placed upon the debtor, he or she isn’t evil, or sinful, or shameful, rather they just failed to meet the terms of their agreed upon terms of repayment and therefore can use this as a method of repayment. <br />	The third essay, which meanders quite a bit, explains what ascetic values are and why they exist. Ascetic values are the opposite of the ancient good. They include poverty, humility, abstinence, and any other “values” which are weakness cloaked as virtue. These arise because the human will would “rather will <i>nothingness</i> than <i>not</i> will”, which is to say those who are powerless still have the will to power, but cannot achieve what is traditionally thought of as powerful, so they change the target of their will to what is powerless. This allows even the weakest of individuals to maintain what Nietzsche sees as essential for humans, the act of willing. These people who can will nothing but nothingness, find solace through the ascetic priest. The ascetic priest, himself a “sick” individual, acts as a doctor, although not in the traditional sense. He doesn’t cure, but rather masks the symptoms of the sick herd—the sick inevitably form a herd, “for one should not overlook this fact: the strong are as naturally inclined to <i>separate</i> as the weak are to <i>congregate</i>”—and only furthers the life turned against itself values which can already be found among the masses. Nietzsche, quite clearly, believes that religion isn’t an appropriate resolve the issues of these ascetic values, but he also doesn’t believe that science does either. Science seems to equally embrace nihilism and takes truth to be its deity instead of a God. Scientists tend to think that they have removed their chains, but for Nietzsche they have simply found a different master. The scientist and the priest are both dispassionate and controlled by either nature or God, respectively, and for Nietzsche both options are unpalatable and are to be done away with. <br /><br /><br /><br />If you enjoyed <i>On the Genealogy of Morals</i> I would recommend the writings and essays of Arthur Schopenhauer (exudes the same self-confidence that Nietzsche does and writes in a similar aphoristic style at times), <i>The Essential Writings of Ralph Waldo Emerson</i> by Ralph Waldo Emerson (if you enjoyed the prosaic, social critique without the diving into definitions and analytics) and <i>Discipline and Punish</i> by Michel Foucault (if it was the unique metalevel analysis of the topic you found interesting). The first two writers were both important influences on Nietzsche and Foucault was influenced by Nietzsche.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12374610766271169797" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9529521-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597771158p2/9529521.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1877239288?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/9529521-brian">Brian</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1877239288" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2392250956054349181">Both the <i>Genealogy</i> and <i>Ecce Homo</i> can be read in one way as Nietzsche's considered assessments of his fellow human beings, largely as cast in relief against his aristocratically alienated conception of himself. Whether or not readers will find his anthropological argument convincing likely hinges upon what conclusions they have managed to draw from their own honest self-analysis.<br/><br/>In reference to this particular volume of the two works presented here, the editorial introductions and footnotes provi</span>
  <span id="freeText2392250956054349181" style="display:none">Both the <i>Genealogy</i> and <i>Ecce Homo</i> can be read in one way as Nietzsche's considered assessments of his fellow human beings, largely as cast in relief against his aristocratically alienated conception of himself. Whether or not readers will find his anthropological argument convincing likely hinges upon what conclusions they have managed to draw from their own honest self-analysis.<br /><br />In reference to this particular volume of the two works presented here, the editorial introductions and footnotes provided by Kaufmann furnish both broadly contextual and passage-specific background information and clarification regarding historical, biographical, and linguistic details which aid the reader in engaging with Nietzsche's thought in something approaching the sense in which he intended it. The translations capture a naturalistic, if declamatory, literary voice, while select citations of the original German reveal in greater depth Nietzsche's rhetorical style and use of wordplay.</span>

<div id="review_189701865" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/189701865" />
    <a title="Nick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3746195-nick"><img alt="Nick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274307381p2/3746195.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/189701865?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick" href="/user/show/3746195-nick">Nick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3746195-nick?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3746195-nick?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3746195-nick?shelf=liberty">liberty</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3746195-nick?shelf=heresy">heresy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3746195-nick?shelf=1880s">1880s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3746195-nick?shelf=1890s">1890s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer189701865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11912126072874864241">Genealogy of morals: After reading "Beyond Good and Evil" this was shockingly clear and lucid. The idea of a pre-christian morality glorifying accomplishment, conflict, strength, etc. being "revolted" against and replaced with a morality of subservience/asceticism is compelling. However, I'd really like to know if his verifiable claims have stove up to the test of time. He makes a lot of linguistic/historical implications about aryans and hebrews which lack citations. Of all the Nietzsche books </span>
  <span id="freeText11912126072874864241" style="display:none">Genealogy of morals: After reading "Beyond Good and Evil" this was shockingly clear and lucid. The idea of a pre-christian morality glorifying accomplishment, conflict, strength, etc. being "revolted" against and replaced with a morality of subservience/asceticism is compelling. However, I'd really like to know if his verifiable claims have stove up to the test of time. He makes a lot of linguistic/historical implications about aryans and hebrews which lack citations. Of all the Nietzsche books I've read (only 3, but still) this has the best balance of engrossing literary prose, and philosophical clarity.<br />4 stars<br /><br />Ecce Homo: Really only worth reading the entire thing if you've read the rest of his books beforehand. Half the book is commentary on his previous books. Also has some zany stuff about climate and diet which was probably my favorite part.<br />3 stars<br /><br />I've gained a lot of understanding and respect for Nietzsche after reading this (esp genealogy). I no longer think of him as a blithering obscurantist. However overall, I still think that Stirner is a superior egoist to Nietzsche. N dog abandons his ego at various points in favor of esseintalist "fixed ideas". Also, I still probably only understood like 60% of what I read, so keep that in mind.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11912126072874864241" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3205729017" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3205729017" />
    <a title="M.a." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26854448-m-a"><img alt="M.a." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1387863511p2/26854448.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3205729017?book_show_action=true">Feb 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="M.a." class="user" itemprop="url" name="M.a." href="/user/show/26854448-m-a">M.a.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3205729017" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4477445618704658090">This is a review of the Genealogy of Morals only.<br/>In this book, Nietzsche debates the values of "good" and "bad", the value of morals as we know it and spins a theory about how these values originated.<br/>Nietzsche claims that man was just like the other animals in the animal kingdom. Among men, there were lions and there were sheep. He calls the lion-types "nobles", "aristocrats", masters" and the sheep-types "commoners" and "slaves".<br/>Of course the masters were happy and content and because they were</span>
  <span id="freeText4477445618704658090" style="display:none">This is a review of the Genealogy of Morals only.<br />In this book, Nietzsche debates the values of "good" and "bad", the value of morals as we know it and spins a theory about how these values originated.<br />Nietzsche claims that man was just like the other animals in the animal kingdom. Among men, there were lions and there were sheep. He calls the lion-types "nobles", "aristocrats", masters" and the sheep-types "commoners" and "slaves".<br />Of course the masters were happy and content and because they were "lions", they were aggressive, arrogant and assertive and they oppressed the "sheep", because that is what lions do.<br />But the slaves were unhappy and resentful and through a "slave revolt" a.k.a. the Judeo-Christian tradition, imposed moral values such as compassion, kindness, and humility on the masters in order to protect their own class. As a result, the wild, magnificent lions became domesticated and sick because of the repression of their instincts and the creation of guilt feelings.<br /><br />Freud, in his book " Civilization and Its Discontents", published in 1930, much later than "The Genealogy of Morals", clearly got his inspiration from Nietzsche<br />when he posited that man agreed to rein in his animal instincts by obeying moral rules in favor of living in a more secure society and that this suppression of instincts creates neurosis.<br /><br />Nietzsche's theory of the origin of the Western notion of good and evil is interesting but it is just a theory. Nietzsche's theory is not more "proven" to be true than the belief that these values originated from the word of God.<br />I actually agree with many of his criticisms of the Church, esp. its hypocrisy, its obsession with guilt and cruel punishments, and the devaluation of this life on earth in favor of the life "to come".<br /><br />What I find extremely objectionable is Nietzsche's unfettered adulation of the values of the "Nobles", and his fanatical hatred of the values of the "Slaves".<br />Here are some examples in his own words:<br /><br />About the "noble" race:<br />"One cannot fail to see at the bottom of all these noble races, the splendid blond beast prowling about avidly in search of spoil and victory; this hidden core needs to erupt from time to time, the animal has to get out again and go back to the wilderness: the Roman, Arabian, Germanic, Japanese nobility, the Homeric heroes, the Scandinavian Vikings- they all shared this need." p.41<br /><br />About the "slave race":<br />" ..but who would not a hundred times sooner fear where one can also admire than NOT fear but be permanently condemned to the repellant sight of the ill-constituted, dwarfed, atrophied, and poisoned? ...<br />Not fear; rather that we no longer have anything to fear in man; that the maggot "man" is swarming in the foreground" p. 43<br /><br />And then, Nietzsche espouses the use of eugenics to promote the "superior" race of "noble" men:<br /><br />"The magnitude of an "advance" can be measured by the mass of things that had to be sacrificed to it; mankind in the mass sacrificed to the prosperity of a single STRONGER species of man- that WOULD be an advance." p.78<br /><br />Such writings no doubt inspired the Nazis in their attempt to purify the "Aryan" race by exterminating all they considered "vermin", especially the Jewish people.<br />The fact that Nietzsche was not an anti-semite, nor particularly pro-German, is irrelevant here.<br />It is the idea itself of encouraging the extermination of masses of "lesser" people for the benefit of an elite of "superior" people, which planted the seeds for genocide.<br /><br />While after WW2 there was a movement which tried to disassociate Nietzsche from the Nazi ideology, more recent scholarship concurs that the Nazis revered Nietzsche and found inspiration in his ideas of a "superior race" and the benefit of sacrificing the masses for its survival. See "Nietzsche and the Nazis" by Stephen R.C. Hicks.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4477445618704658090" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/863686-david?shelf=read-2018">read-2018</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2482154634" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1045526289085778640">[Review only of On the Genealogy of Morals. Kaufmann translation is not listed on its own on Goodreads, so putting my review here. Actually read it as part of the Basic Writings of Nietzsche collection, which is highly recommended.]<br/><br/>My year of finally catching up to Nietzsche probably ends here, with this wonderful work. Honestly, why did I wait so long? It's almost as if Nietzsche has been saddled with a grim and (wildly unfair) Nazi aura just to keep people from reading his playful and funny an</span>
  <span id="freeText1045526289085778640" style="display:none">[Review only of On the Genealogy of Morals. Kaufmann translation is not listed on its own on Goodreads, so putting my review here. Actually read it as part of the Basic Writings of Nietzsche collection, which is highly recommended.]<br /><br />My year of finally catching up to Nietzsche probably ends here, with this wonderful work. Honestly, why did I wait so long? It's almost as if Nietzsche has been saddled with a grim and (wildly unfair) Nazi aura just to keep people from reading his playful and funny and yet deadly serious arguments. (It probably doesn't help that lots of people get steered to Zarathustra first...)<br /><br />It isn't that he's an *easy* read, but he's not the forbidding figure you hear about. Again, he's playful. Insouciant. Irreverent. But, as you read, you can't help shaking your head at how prescient and of-the-moment so much of what he wrote now is revealed to be. Could nothing be more clear than that we exist within the wreckage of a world that failed to reckon with the unmooring that took place under the name "God is Dead"? And yet, the absurd caricatures of what Nietzsche was saying with that phrase, as if he were some boring New Atheist, seem to be all you hear from most people. No, no, it's much worse than all that. But also possibly much better, if we can swerve ourselves around to finding a way to Yes-saying instead of No-saying.<br /><br />Wonderful stuff, a classic for a reason.<br /><br />(I arrived at Nietzsche, finally, to fill in background needed for these fine works: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30072537.The_Prince_of_This_World" title="The Prince of This World by Adam Kotsko" rel="nofollow noopener">The Prince of This World</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1876256.Genesis_and_Apocalypse_A_Theological_Voyage_Toward_Authentic_Christianity" title="Genesis and Apocalypse A Theological Voyage Toward Authentic Christianity by Thomas J.J. Altizer" rel="nofollow noopener">Genesis and Apocalypse: A Theological Voyage Toward Authentic Christianity</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/403846.Caliban_and_the_Witch_Women__the_Body_and_Primitive_Accumulation" title="Caliban and the Witch Women, the Body and Primitive Accumulation by Silvia Federici" rel="nofollow noopener">Caliban and the Witch: Women, the Body and Primitive Accumulation</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1876258.Contemporary_Jesus" title="Contemporary Jesus by Thomas J.J. Altizer" rel="nofollow noopener">Contemporary Jesus</a>.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="1045526289085778640" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2201267326" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2201267326" />
    <a title="C.K. Shaw" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70159200-c-k-shaw"><img alt="C.K. Shaw" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2201267326?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="C.K. Shaw" class="user" itemprop="url" name="C.K. Shaw" href="/user/show/70159200-c-k-shaw">C.K. Shaw</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2201267326" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4898186887118107321"><i>On the Genealogy of Morality</i>, is a fascinating exposition on the development of modern morality. Nietzsche argues that it originated in the ressentiment of the oppressed and that its ubiquity today is the triumph of the morality of the herd over that of the noble. I think Nietzsche’s identification of the nihilism embedded within religious morality (which is also the morality of atheists) is very accurate. Nietzsche skillfully identifies the main issue of human existence: finding and embodying a</span>
  <span id="freeText4898186887118107321" style="display:none"><i>On the Genealogy of Morality</i>, is a fascinating exposition on the development of modern morality. Nietzsche argues that it originated in the ressentiment of the oppressed and that its ubiquity today is the triumph of the morality of the herd over that of the noble. I think Nietzsche’s identification of the nihilism embedded within religious morality (which is also the morality of atheists) is very accurate. Nietzsche skillfully identifies the main issue of human existence: finding and embodying an adequate answer to the problem of suffering. He is relentless in his critique of sub-optimal ways of responding to suffering, condemning religion, science, and most philosophy. <br /><br />The question of what to do with Nietzsche’s insights is a difficult one. Nietzsche argues that it might be optimum for the superior man of the future to work with both moralities. I find modern morality, as espoused by Kant with his categorical imperative, to capture the necessity of taking into account the fact that one must live (now and in the future) among others. I think this necessarily constrains the seemingly infinite possibilities of Nietzsche’s ideal morality, whose only constraint is that it serves life by embodying the will to power. Perhaps, Nietzsche’s critiques can be used to purge whatever ressentiment might be hiding within one’s morality, without throwing out “slave morality” in its entirety. It certainly suggests that one incorporate some of the elements of noble morality, which have been unfairly denigrated out of envy. Lastly, I think Nietzsche provides a good litmus test for the desirability of each moral decision and action in life: Does it affirm life? And does it serve growth and mastery? – I think such a fusion of the slave and master moralities is certainly better than the slave morality alone. <br /><br /><i>Ecce Homo</i> requires more familiarity with Nietzsche’s other works and key ideas, such as eternal recurrence, as he provides his estimation and an overview of the value and roles of those major works and ideas. A less revelatory, but still useful read.<br /><br /><b>A fantastic and life-changing book that I wholeheartedly recommend.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="4898186887118107321" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2025458437?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2017</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2025458437" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10976242513611231849">Classic Nietzsche and a rough picture of his philosophical ideas without much earlier context of Wagner's music, Greek mythology and Schopenhauer. Nietzsche was on his way to explore the ultimate meaning of “good” and the necessity of it. To Nietzsche, the theory, that identifies “good” with “useful” and “practical”, claims that things might be valuable in some highest degree and they are valuable in itself. <br/><br/>Here is some excerpt that I thought would fit the spirit of the GM very well. “We are un</span>
  <span id="freeText10976242513611231849" style="display:none">Classic Nietzsche and a rough picture of his philosophical ideas without much earlier context of Wagner's music, Greek mythology and Schopenhauer. Nietzsche was on his way to explore the ultimate meaning of “good” and the necessity of it. To Nietzsche, the theory, that identifies “good” with “useful” and “practical”, claims that things might be valuable in some highest degree and they are valuable in itself. <br /><br />Here is some excerpt that I thought would fit the spirit of the GM very well. “We are unknown to ourselves, we men of knowledge -- and with good reason. We have never sought ourselves -- how could it happen that we should ever find ourselves? It has rightly been said: ``Where your treasure is, there will your heart be also"; our treasure is where the beehives of our knowledge are. We are constantly making for them, being by nature winged creatures and honey-gathers of the spirit; there is one thing alone we really care about from the heart -- ``bring something home". Whatever else there is in life, so-called ``experiences” — which of us has sufficient earnestness for them? Or sufficient time? Present experience has, I am afraid, always found us ``absent-minded”: we cannot give our hearts to it — not even our ears! Rather, as one divinely preoccupied and immersed in himself into whose ear the bell has just boomed with all its strength the twelve beats of noon suddenly starts up and ask himself:”what really was that which just struck?” So we sometimes rub ourears afterwards and ask, utterly surprised and disconcerted, “what really was that which we have just experienced?” And moreover: “who are we really?”"</span>

          </span>

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_162022'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/162022.Nietzsche?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Nietzsche: Philosopher, Psychologist, Antichrist<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/14824.Walter_Kaufmann?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Walter Kaufmann<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.22 avg rating &mdash; 6,744 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1950\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer15240166583013758419\">This classic is the benchmark against which all modern books about Nietzsche are measured. When Walter Kaufmann wrote it in the immediate aftermath of World War II, most scholars outside Germany viewed Nietzsche as part madman, part proto-Nazi, and a<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15240166583013758419\" style=\"display:none\">This classic is the benchmark against which all modern books about Nietzsche are measured. When Walter Kaufmann wrote it in the immediate aftermath of World War II, most scholars outside Germany viewed Nietzsche as part madman, part proto-Nazi, and almost wholly unphilosophical. Kaufmann rehabilitated Nietzsche nearly single-handedly, presenting his works as one of the great achievements of Western philosophy.\n\nResponding to the powerful myths and countermyths that had sprung up around Nietzsche, Kaufmann offered a patient, evenhanded account of his life and works, and of the uses and abuses to which subsequent generations had put his ideas. Without ignoring or downplaying the ugliness of many of Nietzsche\'s proclamations, he set them in the context of his work as a whole and of the counterexamples yielded by a responsible reading of his books. More positively, he presented Nietzsche\'s ideas about power as one of the great accomplishments of modern philosophy, arguing that his conception of the will to power was not a crude apology for ruthless self-assertion but must be linked to Nietzsche\'s equally profound ideas about sublimation. He also presented Nietzsche as a pioneer of modern psychology and argued that a key to understanding his overall philosophy is to see it as a reaction against Christianity.\n\nMany scholars in the past half century have taken issue with some of Kaufmann\'s interpretations, but the book ranks as one of the most influential accounts ever written of any major Western thinker.<\/span>\n  <a The first part deals with the subject <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17555669168360695019\" style=\"display:none\">In THE CRITIQUE OF JUDGMENT (1790), Immanuel Kant (1724-1804) seeks to establish the a priori principles underlying the faculty of judgment, just as he did in his previous critiques of pure and practical reason. The first part deals with the subject of our aesthetic sensibility; we respond to certain natural phenomena as beautiful, says Kant, when we recognize in nature a harmonious order that satisfies the mind\'s own need for order. The second half of the critique concentrates on the apparent teleology in nature\'s design of organisms. Kant argues that our minds are inclined to see purpose and order in nature and this is the main principle underlying all of our judgments. Although this might imply a super sensible Designer, Kant insists that we cannot prove a supernatural dimension or the existence of God. It begins with a Preface, created after the rest of the manuscript was completed, that explains the core of his method and what sets it apart from any preceding philosophy. The Introduction, written before the rest of the work, summarizes and completes Kant\'s ideas on skepticism by rendering it moot and encouraging idealism and self-realization. The body of the work is divided into six sections of varying length, entitled &quot;Consciousness,&quot; &quot;Self-Consciousness,&quot; &quot;Reason,&quot; &quot;Spirit,&quot; &quot;Religion,&quot; and &quot;Absolute Knowledge.&quot; A myriad of topics are discussed, and explained in such a harmoniously complex way that the method has been termed Hegelian dialectic. Ultimately, the work as a whole is a remarkable study of the mind\'s growth from its direct awareness to scientific philosophy, proving to be a difficult yet highly influential and enduring It is without question Schopenhauer\'s greatest work. Conceived and published before the philosopher was 30 and expanded 25 years later, it is the summation of a lifetime of thought.\n\nFor 70 years, the only unabridged English translation of this work was the Haldane-Kemp collaboration. In 1958, a new translation by E. F. J. Payne appeared that decisively supplanted the older one. Payne\'s translation is superior because it corrects nearly 1,000 errors and omissions in the Haldane-Kemp translation, and it is based on the definitive 1937 German edition of Schopenhauer\'s work prepared by Dr. Arthur Hübscher. Payne\'s edition is the first to translate into English the text\'s many quotations in half a dozen languages. Followi<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13547436590583632235\" style=\"display:none\">Published shortly after his death, the Ethics is undoubtedly Spinoza\'s greatest work - an elegant, fully cohesive cosmology derived from first principles, providing a coherent picture of reality, and a guide to the meaning of an ethical life. Following a logical step-by-step format, it defines in turn the nature of God, the mind, the emotions, human bondage to the emotions, and the power of understanding - moving from a consideration of the eternal, to speculate upon humanity\'s place in the natural order, the nature of freedom and the path to attainable happiness. A powerful work of elegant simplicity, the Ethics is a brilliantly insightful consideration of the possibility of redemption through intense thought and philosophical reflection. The Ethics is presented in the standard translation of the work by Edwin Curley. Published in 1843 and written under the names Johannes de Silentio and Constantine Constantius, respectively, the books demonstrate Kierkegaard\'s transmutation of the personal into the lyrically religious.\n\nEach work uses as a point of departure Kierkegaard\'s breaking of his engagement to Regine Olsen--his sacrifice of &quot;that single individual.&quot; From this beginning &quot;Fear and Trembling&quot; becomes an exploration of the faith that transcends the ethical, as in Abraham\'s willingness to sacrifice his son Isaac at God\'s command. This faith, which persists in the face of the absurd, is rewarded finally by the return of all that the faithful one is willing to sacrifice. &quot;Repetition&quot; discusses the most profound implications of unity of personhood and of identity within change, beginning with the ironic story of a young poet who cannot fulfill the ethical claims of his engagement because of the possible consequences of his marriage. The poet finally despairs of repetition (renewal) in the ethical sphere, as does his advisor and friend Constantius in the aesthetic sphere. The book ends with Constantius\' intimation of a third kind of repetition--in the religious sphere.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"531457615889177918\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));;       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1938.Friedrich_Nietzsche">About Friedrich Nietzsche</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1938.Friedrich_Nietzsche"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1455294131p3/1938.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1938.Friedrich_Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9759559558412454548">Friedrich Wilhelm Nietzsche was a German philosopher of the late 19th century who challenged the foundations of Christianity and traditional morality. He was interested in the enhancement of individual and cultural health, and believed in life, creativity, power, and the realities of the world we live in, rather than those situated in a world beyond. Central to his philosophy is the idea of “life-</span>
  <span id="freeText9759559558412454548" style="display:none">Friedrich Wilhelm Nietzsche was a German philosopher of the late 19th century who challenged the foundations of Christianity and traditional morality. He was interested in the enhancement of individual and cultural health, and believed in life, creativity, power, and the realities of the world we live in, rather than those situated in a world beyond. Central to his philosophy is the idea of “life-affirmation,” which involves an honest questioning of all doctrines that drain life's expansive energies, however socially prevalent those views might be. Often referred to as one of the first existentialist philosophers along with Søren Kierkegaard (1813–1855), Nietzsche's revitalizing philosophy has inspired leading figures in all walks of cultural life, including dancers, poets, novelists, painters, psychologists, philosophers, sociologists and social revolutionaries.<br /><br />From the <a href="http://plato.stanford.edu/entries/nietzsche/" rel="nofollow noopener">Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="9759559558412454548" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
